The front porch campaign allowed Harding to avoid mistakes, and as time dwindled towards the election, his strength grew. The travels of the Democratic candidates eventually caused Harding to make several short speaking tours, but for the most part, he remained in Marion. America had no need for another Wilson, Harding argued, appealing for a president "near the normal".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESinclair163.E2.80.93165_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESinclair163.E2.80.93165-100"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:FDR_and_James_M_Cox_cph.3b03395.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/FDR_and_James_M_Cox_cph.3b03395.jpg/170px-FDR_and_James_M_Cox_cph.3b03395.jpg" width="170" height="199" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/FDR_and_James_M_Cox_cph.3b03395.jpg/255px-FDR_and_James_M_Cox_cph.3b03395.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/FDR_and_James_M_Cox_cph.3b03395.jpg/340px-FDR_and_James_M_Cox_cph.3b03395.jpg 2x" data-file-width="744" data-file-height="873" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:FDR_and_James_M_Cox_cph.3b03395.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Democratic candidates Cox (right) and Roosevelt at a campaign appearance in Washington, DC, 1920</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Harding's vague oratory irritated some; McAdoo described a typical Harding speech as "an army of pompous phrases moving over the landscape in search of an idea. Sometimes these meandering words actually capture a straggling thought and bear it triumphantly, a prisoner in their midst, until it died of servitude and over work."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDean72_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDean72-101"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/H._L._Mencken" title="H. L. Mencken">H. L. Mencken</a> concurred, "it reminds me of a string of wet sponges, it reminds me of tattered washing on the line; it reminds me of stale bean soup, of college yells, of dogs barking idiotically through endless nights. It is so bad that a kind of grandeur creeps into it. It drags itself out of the dark abysm&#160;... of pish, and crawls insanely up the topmost pinnacle of tosh. It is rumble and bumble. It is balder and dash.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102"><span>[</span>d<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDean72_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDean72-101"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup> <i>The New York Times</i> took a more positive view of Harding's speeches, stating that in them the majority of people could find "a reflection of their own indeterminate thoughts".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESinclair166_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESinclair166-103"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Wilson had stated that the 1920 election would be a "great and solemn referendum" on the League of Nations, making it difficult for Cox to maneuver on the issue—although Roosevelt strongly supported the League, Cox was less enthusiastic.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray_196943.E2.80.9345_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray_196943.E2.80.9345-104"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup> Harding opposed entry into the League of Nations as negotiated by Wilson, but favored an "association of nations",<sup id="cite_ref-anb_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-anb-24"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup> based on the <a href="/wiki/Permanent_Court_of_Arbitration" title="Permanent Court of Arbitration">Permanent Court of Arbitration</a> at <a href="/wiki/The_Hague" title="The Hague">The Hague</a>. This was general enough to satisfy most Republicans, and only a few bolted the party over this issue. By October, Cox had realized there was widespread public opposition to Article X, and stated that <a href="/wiki/Reservation_(law)" title="Reservation (law)">reservations</a> to the treaty might be necessary; this shift allowed Harding to say no more on the subject.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson27.E2.80.9328_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson27.E2.80.9328-105"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:WGHarding.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/WGHarding.jpg/170px-WGHarding.jpg" width="170" height="237" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/WGHarding.jpg/255px-WGHarding.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/WGHarding.jpg/340px-WGHarding.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2605" data-file-height="3625" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:WGHarding.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Warren G. Harding campaigning in 1920</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The RNC hired <a href="/wiki/Albert_Lasker" title="Albert Lasker">Albert Lasker</a>, an advertising executive from Chicago, to publicize Harding, and Lasker unleashed a broad-based advertising campaign that used many now-standard advertising techniques for the first time in a presidential campaign. Lasker's approach included newsreels and sound recordings. Visitors to Marion had their photographs taken with Senator and Mrs. Harding, and copies were sent to their hometown newspapers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDean69_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDean69-106"><span>[</span>102<span>]</span></a></sup> Billboard posters, newspapers and magazines were employed in addition to motion pictures. <a href="/wiki/Telemarketers" title="Telemarketers" class="mw-redirect">Telemarketers</a> were used to make phone calls with scripted dialogues to promote Harding.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorello64.E2.80.9365_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorello64.E2.80.9365-107"><span>[</span>103<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The rumors that the Hardings were of partially African American blood surfaced again in the last days of the campaign, promoted by <a href="/wiki/Wooster_College" title="Wooster College" class="mw-redirect">Wooster College</a> professor <a href="/wiki/William_Estabrook_Chancellor" title="William Estabrook Chancellor">William Estabrook Chancellor</a>. Once these claims became known, reporters filed lengthy stories on them from Marion, which their editors declined to print. The Harding campaign refused to acknowledge or respond to the allegations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussell412.E2.80.93416_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussell412.E2.80.93416-108"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>By Election Day, November 2, 1920, few had any doubts that the Republican ticket would win.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray_196962_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray_196962-109"><span>[</span>105<span>]</span></a></sup> Harding received 60.2&#160;percent of the popular vote, the highest percentage since the evolution of the <a href="/wiki/Two-party_system" title="Two-party system">two-party system</a>, and 404 <a href="/wiki/Electoral_College_(United_States)" title="Electoral College (United States)">electoral votes</a>. Cox received 34&#160;percent of the national vote and 127 electoral votes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussell418_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussell418-110"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup> Campaigning from a federal prison where he was serving a sentence for opposing the war, <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Party_of_America" title="Socialist Party of America">Socialist</a> <a href="/wiki/Eugene_V._Debs" title="Eugene V. Debs">Eugene V. Debs</a> received 3&#160;percent of the national vote. The Republicans greatly increased their majority in each house of Congress.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussell420_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussell420-111"><span>[</span>107<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray_196966_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray_196966-112"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="President_.281921.E2.80.931923.29">President (1921–1923)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Warren_G._Harding&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: President (1921–1923)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wharding.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Wharding.jpeg/170px-Wharding.jpeg" width="170" height="270" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Wharding.jpeg/255px-Wharding.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Wharding.jpeg/340px-Wharding.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="1864" data-file-height="2956" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wharding.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<center>President Warren G. Harding<br />
1921–1923<br />
Official White House Portrait</center>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Warren_G._Harding" title="Presidency of Warren G. Harding">Presidency of Warren G. Harding</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inauguration_and_appointments">Inauguration and appointments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Warren_G._Harding&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Inauguration and appointments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Inauguration_of_Warren_G._Harding" title="Inauguration of Warren G. Harding">Inauguration of Warren G. Harding</a></div>
<p>Warren Harding was sworn in as president on March 4, 1921, in the presence of his wife and father. Harding preferred a low-key inauguration, without the customary parade, leaving only the swearing-in ceremony and a brief reception at the White House. In his inaugural address he declared, "Our most dangerous tendency is to expect too much from the government and at the same time do too little for it."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussell2.2C14_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussell2.2C14-113"><span>[</span>109<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>After the election, Harding had announced he was going on vacation, and that no decisions about appointments would be made until he returned to Marion in December. He went to Texas, where he fished and played golf with his friend <a href="/wiki/Frank_Scobey" title="Frank Scobey" class="mw-redirect">Frank Scobey</a> (soon to be <a href="/wiki/Director_of_the_Mint" title="Director of the Mint" class="mw-redirect">Director of the Mint</a>), then took ship for the <a href="/wiki/Panama_Canal_Zone" title="Panama Canal Zone">Panama Canal Zone</a>. He went to Washington, where he was given a hero's welcome<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114"><span>[</span>e<span>]</span></a></sup> when Congress opened in early December as the first sitting senator to be elected to the White House. Back in Ohio, he planned to consult the "best minds" of the country on appointments, and they duly journeyed to Marion to offer their counsel.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussell420.E2.80.93424_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussell420.E2.80.93424-115"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESinclair181_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESinclair181-116"><span>[</span>111<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Harding chose pro-League Charles Evans Hughes as his Secretary of State, ignoring advice from Senator Lodge and others. After <a href="/wiki/Charles_G._Dawes" title="Charles G. Dawes">Charles G. Dawes</a> declined the Treasury position, Harding asked Pittsburgh banker <a href="/wiki/Andrew_W._Mellon" title="Andrew W. Mellon" class="mw-redirect">Andrew W. Mellon</a>, one of the richest men in the country; he agreed. Harding appointed Herbert Hoover as <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_Commerce" title="United States Secretary of Commerce">United States Secretary of Commerce</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson38.E2.80.9339_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson38.E2.80.9339-117"><span>[</span>112<span>]</span></a></sup> RNC Chairman Will Hays was made <a href="/wiki/United_States_Postmaster_General" title="United States Postmaster General">Postmaster General</a>, then a cabinet post; he would leave after a year in the position to become chief censor to the motion picture industry.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDean89_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDean89-118"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The two Harding cabinet appointees who darkened the reputation of his administration for their involvement in scandal were Harding's Senate friend, <a href="/wiki/Albert_B._Fall" title="Albert B. Fall">Albert B. Fall</a> of New Mexico, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_the_Interior" title="United States Secretary of the Interior">Interior Secretary</a>, and Daugherty, who became Attorney General. Fall was a Western rancher and former miner, and was pro-development.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDean89_118-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDean89-118"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup> He was opposed by conservationists such as <a href="/wiki/Gifford_Pinchot" title="Gifford Pinchot">Gifford Pinchot</a>, who wrote, "it would have been possible to pick a worse man for Secretary of the Interior, but not altogether easy".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENoggle242_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENoggle242-119"><span>[</span>114<span>]</span></a></sup> <i>The New York Times</i> mocked the Daugherty appointment, stating that rather than select one of the best minds, Harding had been content "to choose merely a best friend".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESinclair188_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESinclair188-120"><span>[</span>115<span>]</span></a></sup> Eugene P. Trani and David L. Wilson, in their volume on Harding's presidency, suggest that the appointment made sense then, since Daugherty was "a competent lawyer well-acquainted with the seamy side of politics&#160;... a first-class political troubleshooter and someone Harding could trust".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson43_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson43-121"><span>[</span>116<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Harding_swearing_in.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Harding_swearing_in.jpg/220px-Harding_swearing_in.jpg" width="220" height="214" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Harding_swearing_in.jpg/330px-Harding_swearing_in.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Harding_swearing_in.jpg/440px-Harding_swearing_in.jpg 2x" data-file-width="520" data-file-height="506" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Harding_swearing_in.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Inauguration of Warren G. Harding, March 4, 1921</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a href="/wiki/File:President_Warren_G._Harding%27s_First_Cabinet_1921.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/President_Warren_G._Harding%27s_First_Cabinet_1921.jpg/440px-President_Warren_G._Harding%27s_First_Cabinet_1921.jpg" width="440" height="203" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/President_Warren_G._Harding%27s_First_Cabinet_1921.jpg/660px-President_Warren_G._Harding%27s_First_Cabinet_1921.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/President_Warren_G._Harding%27s_First_Cabinet_1921.jpg/880px-President_Warren_G._Harding%27s_First_Cabinet_1921.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1845" data-file-height="853" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:President_Warren_G._Harding%27s_First_Cabinet_1921.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<center>President Harding's first Cabinet, 1921</center>
</div>
</div>
</div>

<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_policy">Foreign policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Warren_G._Harding&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Foreign policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Formally_ending_the_war_and_European_relations">Formally ending the war and European relations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Warren_G._Harding&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Formally ending the war and European relations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Harding made it clear when he appointed Hughes as Secretary of State that the former justice would run foreign policy, a change from Wilson's close management of international affairs.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussell43_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussell43-122"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup> Hughes had to work within some broad outlines; after taking office, Harding hardened his stance on the League of Nations, deciding the U.S. would not join even a scaled-down version of the League. With the Treaty of Versailles unratified by the Senate, the U.S. remained technically at war with Germany, Austria, and Hungary. Peacemaking began with the <a href="/wiki/Knox%E2%80%93Porter_Resolution" title="Knox–Porter Resolution">Knox–Porter Resolution</a>, declaring the U.S. at peace and reserving any rights granted under Versailles. Treaties <a href="/wiki/U.S.%E2%80%93German_Peace_Treaty_(1921)" title="U.S.–German Peace Treaty (1921)">with Germany</a>, <a href="/wiki/U.S.%E2%80%93Austrian_Peace_Treaty_(1921)" title="U.S.–Austrian Peace Treaty (1921)">Austria</a> and <a href="/wiki/U.S.%E2%80%93Hungarian_Peace_Treaty_(1921)" title="U.S.–Hungarian Peace Treaty (1921)">Hungary</a>, each containing many of the non-League provisions of the Treaty of Versailles, were ratified in 1921.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson142.E2.80.93145_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson142.E2.80.93145-123"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>This still left the question of relations between the U.S. and the League. Hughes' State Department initially ignored communications from the League, or tried to bypass it through direct dealings with member nations. By 1922, though, the U.S., through its consul in Geneva, was dealing with the League, and though the U.S. refused to participate in any meeting with political implications, it sent observers to sessions dealing with technical and humanitarian matters.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson145.E2.80.93147_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson145.E2.80.93147-124"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>By the time Harding took office, there were calls from foreign governments for reduction of the massive war debt owed to the United States, and the German government sought to reduce the reparations that it was required to pay. The U.S. refused to consider any multilateral settlement. Harding sought passage of a plan proposed by Mellon to give the administration broad authority to reduce war debts in negotiation, but Congress in 1922 passed a more restrictive bill. Hughes negotiated an agreement for Britain to pay off its war debt over 62 years at low interest, effectively reducing the <a href="/wiki/Present_value" title="Present value">present value</a> of the obligations. This agreement, approved by Congress in 1923, set a pattern for negotiations with other nations. Talks with Germany on reduction of reparations payments would in 1924 result in the <a href="/wiki/Dawes_plan" title="Dawes plan" class="mw-redirect">Dawes plan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson162.E2.80.93163_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson162.E2.80.93163-125"><span>[</span>120<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>A pressing issue not resolved by Wilson was the question of policy towards <a href="/wiki/Bolshevik" title="Bolshevik" class="mw-redirect">Bolshevik</a> Russia. The U.S. had been among the nations that had sent troops there after the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Revolution" title="Russian Revolution">Russian Revolution</a>. Afterwards, Wilson refused to recognize Russia. Under Harding, Commerce Secretary Hoover, with considerable experience of Russian affairs, took the lead on policy. When <a href="/wiki/Russian_famine_of_1921" title="Russian famine of 1921">famine struck Russia in 1921</a>, Hoover had the <a href="/wiki/American_Relief_Administration" title="American Relief Administration">American Relief Administration</a>, which he had headed, negotiate with the Russians to provide aid. Soviet leaders (the <a href="/wiki/U.S.S.R." title="U.S.S.R." class="mw-redirect">U.S.S.R.</a> was established in 1922) hoped in vain that the agreement would lead to recognition. Hoover supported trade with Russia, fearing U.S. companies would be frozen out of the Soviet market, but Hughes opposed this, and the matter was not resolved under Harding's presidency.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson116.E2.80.93126_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson116.E2.80.93126-126"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Disarmament">Disarmament</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Warren_G._Harding&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Disarmament">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Washington_Naval_Conference" title="Washington Naval Conference">Washington Naval Conference</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_Evans_Hughes-01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Charles_Evans_Hughes-01.jpg/170px-Charles_Evans_Hughes-01.jpg" width="170" height="235" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Charles_Evans_Hughes-01.jpg/255px-Charles_Evans_Hughes-01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Charles_Evans_Hughes-01.jpg/340px-Charles_Evans_Hughes-01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="679" data-file-height="940" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_Evans_Hughes-01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Charles_Evans_Hughes" title="Charles Evans Hughes">Charles Evans Hughes</a>, former Supreme Court justice and Harding's Secretary of State</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Harding had urged disarmament, and lower defense costs, during the campaign, but it had not been a major issue. He gave a speech to a joint session of Congress in April 1921, setting out his legislative priorities. Among the few foreign policy matters he mentioned was disarmament, with the president stating that the government could not "be unmindful of the call for reduced expenditure" on defense.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson149.E2.80.93150_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson149.E2.80.93150-127"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Idaho Senator <a href="/wiki/William_Borah" title="William Borah">William Borah</a> had proposed a conference at which the major naval powers, the U.S., Britain, and Japan, would agree to cuts in their fleets. Harding concurred, and after some diplomatic discussions, representatives of nine nations convened in Washington in November 1921. Most of the diplomats first attended <a href="/wiki/Veterans_Day" title="Veterans Day">Armistice Day</a> ceremonies at <a href="/wiki/Arlington_National_Cemetery" title="Arlington National Cemetery">Arlington National Cemetery</a>, where Harding spoke at the entombment of the <a href="/wiki/Tomb_of_the_Unknowns" title="Tomb of the Unknowns">Unknown Soldier of World War I</a>, whose identity, "took flight with his imperishable soul. We know not whence he came, only that his death marks him with the everlasting glory of an American dying for his country".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDean130.E2.80.93131_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDean130.E2.80.93131-128"><span>[</span>123<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Hughes, in his speech at the opening session of the conference on November 12, 1921, made the American proposal—the U.S would decommission or not build 30 warships if Great Britain did the same for 19 vessels, and Japan 17 ships.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussell481_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussell481-129"><span>[</span>124<span>]</span></a></sup> The secretary was generally successful, and agreements were reached on this and other points, including settlements to disputes over islands in the Pacific, and limitations on the use of poison gas. The naval agreement was limited to battleships and to some extent aircraft carriers, and in the end did not prevent rearmament. Nevertheless, Harding and Hughes were widely applauded in the press for their work. Harding had appointed Senator Lodge and the <a href="/wiki/Senate_Minority_Leader" title="Senate Minority Leader" class="mw-redirect">Senate Minority Leader</a>, Alabama's <a href="/wiki/Oscar_Underwood" title="Oscar Underwood">Oscar Underwood</a>, to the U.S. delegation; they helped ensure that the treaties made it through the Senate mostly unscathed, though that body added reservations to some of the treaties.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESinclair241.E2.80.93245_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESinclair241.E2.80.93245-130"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDean132.E2.80.93134_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDean132.E2.80.93134-131"><span>[</span>126<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The U.S. had acquired over a thousand vessels during World War I, and still owned most of them when Harding took office. Congress had authorized their disposal <a href="/wiki/Merchant_Marine_Act_of_1920" title="Merchant Marine Act of 1920">in 1920</a>, but the Senate would not confirm Wilson's nominees to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Shipping_Board" title="United States Shipping Board">Shipping Board</a>. Harding appointed Albert Lasker as its chairman; the advertising executive undertook to run the fleet as profitably as possible until it could be sold. Most ships proved impossible to sell at anything approaching the government's cost. Lasker recommended a large subsidy to the <a href="/wiki/Merchant_marine" title="Merchant marine" class="mw-redirect">merchant marine</a> to enable the sales, and Harding repeatedly urged Congress to enact it. Unpopular in the Midwest, the bill passed the House but was defeated by a <a href="/wiki/Filibuster" title="Filibuster">filibuster</a> in the Senate, and most government ships were eventually scrapped.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson174.E2.80.93178_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson174.E2.80.93178-132"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Latin_America">Latin America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Warren_G._Harding&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Latin America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Intervention in Latin America had been a minor campaign issue; Harding spoke against Wilson's decision to send U.S. troops to the Dominican Republic and Haiti, and attacked the Democratic vice presidential candidate, Franklin Roosevelt, for his role in the Haitian intervention. Once Harding was sworn in, Hughes worked to improve relations with Latin American countries who were wary of the American use of the <a href="/wiki/Monroe_Doctrine" title="Monroe Doctrine">Monroe Doctrine</a> to justify intervention; at the time of Harding's inauguration, the U.S. also had troops in Cuba and Nicaragua. The troops stationed in Cuba to protect American interests were withdrawn in 1921; U.S. forces remained in the other three nations through Harding's presidency.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133"><span>[</span>f<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson133.E2.80.93135_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson133.E2.80.93135-134"><span>[</span>128<span>]</span></a></sup> In April 1921, Harding gained the ratification of the <a href="/wiki/Thomson%E2%80%93Urrutia_Treaty" title="Thomson–Urrutia Treaty">Thomson–Urrutia Treaty</a> with Colombia, granting that nation $25,000,000 as settlement for the U.S.-provoked <a href="/wiki/Separation_of_Panama_from_Colombia" title="Separation of Panama from Colombia">Panamanian revolution of 1903</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969340.E2.80.93341_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969340.E2.80.93341-135"><span>[</span>129<span>]</span></a></sup> The Latin American nations were not fully satisfied, as the U.S. refused to renounce interventionism, though Hughes pledged to limit it to nations near the Panama Canal and to make it clear what the U.S. aims were.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson136.E2.80.93137_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson136.E2.80.93137-136"><span>[</span>130<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The U.S. had intervened repeatedly in Mexico under Wilson, and had withdrawn diplomatic recognition, setting conditions for reinstatement. The Mexican government under President <a href="/wiki/%C3%81lvaro_Obreg%C3%B3n" title="Álvaro Obregón">Álvaro Obregón</a> wanted recognition before negotiations, but Wilson and his Secretary of State, <a href="/wiki/Bainbridge_Colby" title="Bainbridge Colby">Bainbridge Colby</a>, refused. Both Hughes and Fall opposed recognition; Hughes instead sent a draft treaty to the Mexicans in May 1921, which included pledges to reimburse Americans for losses in Mexico since the <a href="/wiki/Mexican_Revolution" title="Mexican Revolution">1910 revolution</a> there. Obregón was unwilling to sign a treaty before being recognized, and worked to improve the relationship between American business and Mexico, reaching agreement with creditors, and mounting a public relations campaign in the United States. This had its effect, and by mid-1922, Fall was less influential than he had been, lessening the resistance to recognition. The two presidents appointed commissioners to reach a deal, and the U.S. recognized the Obregón government on August 31, 1923, just under a month after Harding's death, substantially on the terms proffered by Mexico.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson130.E2.80.93132_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson130.E2.80.93132-137"><span>[</span>131<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Domestic_policy">Domestic policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Warren_G._Harding&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Domestic policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Postwar_recession_and_recovery">Postwar recession and recovery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Warren_G._Harding&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Postwar recession and recovery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Depression_of_1920%E2%80%9321" title="Depression of 1920–21">Depression of 1920–21</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chas_G_Dawes-H%26E.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Chas_G_Dawes-H%26E.jpg/170px-Chas_G_Dawes-H%26E.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Chas_G_Dawes-H%26E.jpg/255px-Chas_G_Dawes-H%26E.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Chas_G_Dawes-H%26E.jpg/340px-Chas_G_Dawes-H%26E.jpg 2x" data-file-width="569" data-file-height="761" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chas_G_Dawes-H%26E.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Charles_Dawes" title="Charles Dawes" class="mw-redirect">Charles Dawes</a>—the first budget director and later, vice president under Coolidge</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>When Harding took office on March 4, 1921, the nation was in the midst of <a href="/wiki/Depression_of_1920%E2%80%9321" title="Depression of 1920–21">a postwar economic decline</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray_197340.E2.80.9341_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray_197340.E2.80.9341-138"><span>[</span>132<span>]</span></a></sup> At the suggestion of its leaders, Harding called a special session of Congress to convene on April 11. When Harding addressed the joint session the following day, he urged the reduction of income taxes (raised during the war), an increase in tariffs on agricultural goods to protect the American farmer, as well as more wide-ranging reforms, such as support for highways, aviation, and radio.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson54.E2.80.9357_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson54.E2.80.9357-139"><span>[</span>133<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray_197352.E2.80.9355_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray_197352.E2.80.9355-140"><span>[</span>134<span>]</span></a></sup> But it was not until May 27 that Congress passed an emergency tariff increase on agricultural products. <a href="/wiki/Budget_and_Accounting_Act_of_1921" title="Budget and Accounting Act of 1921" class="mw-redirect">An act</a> authorizing a <a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_the_Budget" title="Bureau of the Budget" class="mw-redirect">Bureau of the Budget</a> followed on June 10; Harding appointed Charles Dawes as bureau director with a mandate to cut expenditures.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray_197351.E2.80.9352_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray_197351.E2.80.9352-141"><span>[</span>135<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Treasury Secretary Mellon also recommended to Congress that income tax rates be cut. He asked that the <a href="/wiki/Excess_profits_tax" title="Excess profits tax">excess profits tax</a> on corporations be abolished. The <a href="/wiki/House_Ways_and_Means_Committee" title="House Ways and Means Committee" class="mw-redirect">House Ways and Means Committee</a> endorsed Mellon's proposals, but some congressmen, who wanted to raise tax rates on corporations, fought the measure. Harding was unsure what side to endorse, telling a friend, "I can't make a damn thing out of this tax problem. I listen to one side, and they seem right, and then—God!—I talk to the other side, and they seem just as right."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray_197352.E2.80.9355_140-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray_197352.E2.80.9355-140"><span>[</span>134<span>]</span></a></sup> Harding tried compromise, and gained passage of the bill in the House after the end of the excess profits tax was delayed a year. In the Senate, the tax bill became entangled in efforts to vote World War I veterans a soldier's bonus. Frustrated by the delays, on July 12, Harding appeared before the Senate and urged it to pass the tax legislation without the bonus. It was not until November that <a href="/wiki/Revenue_Act_of_1921" title="Revenue Act of 1921">the bill</a> finally passed, with higher rates than Mellon had proposed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray_197355.E2.80.9358_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray_197355.E2.80.9358-142"><span>[</span>136<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDean108_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDean108-143"><span>[</span>137<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:AWMellon.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/AWMellon.jpg/170px-AWMellon.jpg" width="170" height="224" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/AWMellon.jpg/255px-AWMellon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/AWMellon.jpg/340px-AWMellon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="3158" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:AWMellon.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Secretary of the Treasury <a href="/wiki/Andrew_W._Mellon" title="Andrew W. Mellon" class="mw-redirect">Andrew W. Mellon</a> advocated lower tax rates.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Harding had opposed payment of a bonus to veterans, arguing in his Senate address that much was already being done for them by a grateful nation, and that the bill would "break down our Treasury, from which so much is later on to be expected."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDean107.E2.80.93108_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDean107.E2.80.93108-144"><span>[</span>138<span>]</span></a></sup> The Senate sent the bonus bill back to committee,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDean107.E2.80.93108_144-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDean107.E2.80.93108-144"><span>[</span>138<span>]</span></a></sup> but the issue returned when Congress reconvened in December 1921. A bill providing a bonus, without a means of funding it, was passed by both houses in September 1922. Harding vetoed it, and the veto was narrowly sustained. <a href="/wiki/World_War_Adjusted_Compensation_Act" title="World War Adjusted Compensation Act">A bonus</a>, not payable in cash, was voted to soldiers despite Coolidge's veto in 1924.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson78.E2.80.9379_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson78.E2.80.9379-145"><span>[</span>139<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In his first <a href="/wiki/State_of_the_Union" title="State of the Union">annual message to Congress</a>, Harding sought the power to adjust tariff rates. The passage of the tariff bill in the Senate, and in <a href="/wiki/Conference_committee" title="Conference committee" class="mw-redirect">conference committee</a> became a feeding frenzy of lobbyist interests.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson74.E2.80.9375_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson74.E2.80.9375-146"><span>[</span>140<span>]</span></a></sup> Harding, when he enacted the <a href="/wiki/Fordney%E2%80%93McCumber_Tariff" title="Fordney–McCumber Tariff">Fordney–McCumber Tariff</a> Act on September 21, 1922, made a brief <a href="/wiki/Signing_statement" title="Signing statement">signing statement</a>, praising only that the bill gave him some power to adjust rates.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDean104_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDean104-147"><span>[</span>141<span>]</span></a></sup> According to Trani and Wilson, the bill was "ill-considered. It wrought havoc in international commerce and made the repayment of war debts more difficult."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson74_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson74-148"><span>[</span>142<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Embracing_new_technologies">Embracing new technologies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Warren_G._Harding&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Embracing new technologies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The 1920s were a time of modernization for America. Use of electricity became increasingly common. Mass production of the motor car stimulated other industries, as well, such as highway construction, rubber, steel, and building, as hotels were erected to accommodate the tourists venturing upon the roads. This economic boost helped bring the nation out of the recession.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESinclair206_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESinclair206-149"><span>[</span>143<span>]</span></a></sup> To improve and expand the nation's highway system, Harding signed the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Aid_Highway_Act_of_1921_(Phipps_Act)" title="Federal Aid Highway Act of 1921 (Phipps Act)" class="mw-redirect">Federal Highway Act of 1921</a>. From 1921 to 1923, the federal government spent $162&#160;million on America's highway system, infusing the U.S. economy with a large amount of capital.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWynn217.E2.80.93218_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWynn217.E2.80.93218-150"><span>[</span>144<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1922, Harding proclaimed that America was in the age of the "motor car", which "reflects our standard of living and gauges the speed of our present-day life."<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151"><span>[</span>145<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Harding had urged regulation of radio broadcasting in his April 1921 speech to Congress.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray_197346_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray_197346-152"><span>[</span>146<span>]</span></a></sup> Commerce Secretary Hoover took charge of this project, and convened a conference of radio broadcasters in 1922, which led to a voluntary agreement for licensing of <a href="/wiki/Radio_frequency" title="Radio frequency">radio frequencies</a> through the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Commerce" title="United States Department of Commerce">Commerce Department</a>. Both Harding and Hoover realized something more than an agreement was needed, but Congress was slow to act, not imposing radio regulation until 1927.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson88_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson88-153"><span>[</span>147<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Harding also wished to promote aviation, and Hoover again took the lead, convening a national conference on commercial aviation. The discussions focused on safety matters, inspection of airplanes, and licensing of pilots. Harding again promoted legislation but nothing was done until 1926, when the <a href="/wiki/Air_Commerce_Act_of_1926" title="Air Commerce Act of 1926" class="mw-redirect">Air Commerce Act</a> created the <a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Aeronautics" title="Bureau of Aeronautics">Bureau of Aeronautics</a> within Hoover's Commerce Department.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson88_153-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson88-153"><span>[</span>147<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Business_and_labor">Business and labor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Warren_G._Harding&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Business and labor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Great_Railroad_Strike_of_1922" title="Great Railroad Strike of 1922">Great Railroad Strike of 1922</a></div>
<p>Harding's attitude toward business was that government should aid it as much as possible.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson83_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson83-154"><span>[</span>148<span>]</span></a></sup> He was suspicious of <a href="/wiki/Organized_labor" title="Organized labor" class="mw-redirect">organized labor</a>, viewing it as a conspiracy against business.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESinclair253.E2.80.93254_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESinclair253.E2.80.93254-155"><span>[</span>149<span>]</span></a></sup> He sought to get them to work together at a conference on unemployment that he called to meet in September 1921 at Hoover's recommendation. Harding warned in his opening address that no federal money would be available. No important legislation came as a result, though some <a href="/wiki/Public_works" title="Public works">public works</a> projects were speeded up.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson92.E2.80.9393_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson92.E2.80.9393-156"><span>[</span>150<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Within broad limits, Harding allowed each cabinet secretary to run his department as he saw fit.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray_197329_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray_197329-157"><span>[</span>151<span>]</span></a></sup> Hoover expanded the Commerce Department to make it more useful to business. This was consistent with Hoover's view that the private sector should take the lead in managing the economy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson84_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson84-158"><span>[</span>152<span>]</span></a></sup> Harding greatly respected his Commerce Secretary, often asked his advice, and backed him to the hilt, calling Hoover "the smartest '<a href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/gink" class="extiw" title="wiktionary:gink">gink</a>' I know"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray_197332.E2.80.9333_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray_197332.E2.80.9333-159"><span>[</span>153<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Widespread strikes marked 1922, as labor sought redress for falling wages and increased unemployment. In April, 500,000 coal miners, led by <a href="/wiki/John_L._Lewis" title="John L. Lewis">John L. Lewis</a>, struck over wage cuts. Mining executives argued that the industry was seeing hard times; Lewis accused them of trying to break the union. As the strike became protracted, Harding offered compromise to settle it. As Harding proposed, the miners agreed to return to work, and Congress created a commission to look into their grievances.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson97.E2.80.9399_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson97.E2.80.9399-160"><span>[</span>154<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On July 1, 1922, 400,000 railroad workers went on strike. Harding proposed a settlement that made some concessions, but management objected. Attorney General Daugherty convinced Judge <a href="/wiki/James_H._Wilkerson" title="James H. Wilkerson" class="mw-redirect">James H. Wilkerson</a> to issue a sweeping injunction to break up the strike. Although there was public support for the Wilkerson injunction, Harding felt it went too far, and had Daugherty and Wilkerson amend it. The injunction succeeded in ending the strike; however, tensions remained high between railroad workers and management for years.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussell546.E2.80.93549_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussell546.E2.80.93549-161"><span>[</span>155<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>By 1922, the <a href="/wiki/Eight-hour_day" title="Eight-hour day">eight-hour day</a> had become common in American industry. One exception was in <a href="/wiki/Steel_mills" title="Steel mills" class="mw-redirect">steel mills</a>, where workers labored through a twelve-hour workday, seven days a week. Hoover considered this practice barbaric, and got Harding to convene a conference of steel manufacturers with a view to ending it. The conference established a committee under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/U._S._Steel" title="U. S. Steel" class="mw-redirect">U. S. Steel</a> chairman <a href="/wiki/Elbert_Gary" title="Elbert Gary" class="mw-redirect">Elbert Gary</a>, which in early 1923 recommended against ending the practice. Harding sent a letter to Gary deploring the result, which was printed in the press, and public outcry caused the manufacturers to reverse themselves and standardize the eight-hour day.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESinclair255.E2.80.93256_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESinclair255.E2.80.93256-162"><span>[</span>156<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_rights_and_immigration">Civil rights and immigration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Warren_G._Harding&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Civil rights and immigration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Harding_at_Birmingham.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Harding_at_Birmingham.jpg/220px-Harding_at_Birmingham.jpg" width="220" height="204" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Harding_at_Birmingham.jpg/330px-Harding_at_Birmingham.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Harding_at_Birmingham.jpg/440px-Harding_at_Birmingham.jpg 2x" data-file-width="535" data-file-height="496" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Harding_at_Birmingham.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Harding addresses the segregated crowd in Birmingham, October 26, 1921</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Although Harding's first address to Congress called for passage of anti-lynching legislation,<sup id="cite_ref-Baker18Aug_7-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baker18Aug-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> he initially seemed inclined to do no more for African Americans than had Republican presidents of the recent past; he asked cabinet officers to find places for blacks in their departments. Sinclair suggested that the fact that Harding received two-fifths of the Southern vote in 1920 led him to see political opportunity for his party in the <a href="/wiki/Solid_South" title="Solid South">Solid South</a>. On October 26, 1921, Harding gave a speech in <a href="/wiki/Birmingham,_Alabama" title="Birmingham, Alabama">Birmingham, Alabama</a>, to a segregated audience of 20,000 whites and 10,000 blacks. Harding, while stating that the social and racial differences between whites and blacks could not be bridged, urged equal political rights for the African American. Many African Americans at that time voted Republican, especially in the Democratic South, and Harding stated he did not mind seeing that support end if the result was a strong two-party system in the South. He was willing to see <a href="/wiki/Literacy_test" title="Literacy test">literacy tests</a> for voting continue, if applied fairly to white and black.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESinclair230.E2.80.93234_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESinclair230.E2.80.93234-163"><span>[</span>157<span>]</span></a></sup> "Whether you like it or not," Harding told his segregated audience, "unless our democracy is a lie, you must stand for that equality."<sup id="cite_ref-Baker18Aug_7-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baker18Aug-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> The white section of the audience listened in silence while the black section cheered.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164"><span>[</span>158<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Taft_Harding_Robert_Lincoln_1922.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Taft_Harding_Robert_Lincoln_1922.jpg/220px-Taft_Harding_Robert_Lincoln_1922.jpg" width="220" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Taft_Harding_Robert_Lincoln_1922.jpg/330px-Taft_Harding_Robert_Lincoln_1922.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Taft_Harding_Robert_Lincoln_1922.jpg/440px-Taft_Harding_Robert_Lincoln_1922.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4970" data-file-height="3776" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Taft_Harding_Robert_Lincoln_1922.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Harding (center) with Chief Justice <a href="/wiki/William_Howard_Taft" title="William Howard Taft">Taft</a> (left) and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Lincoln" title="Robert Lincoln" class="mw-redirect">Robert Lincoln</a> at the dedication of the <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_Memorial" title="Lincoln Memorial">Lincoln Memorial</a>, May 30, 1922</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Harding had spoken out against lynching in his April 1921 speech before Congress and supported Congressman <a href="/wiki/Leonidas_Dyer" title="Leonidas Dyer" class="mw-redirect">Leonidas Dyer</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Dyer_Anti-Lynching_Bill" title="Dyer Anti-Lynching Bill">federal anti-lynching bill</a>, which passed the House of Representatives in January 1922.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDean123_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDean123-165"><span>[</span>159<span>]</span></a></sup> When it reached the Senate floor in November 1922, it was filibustered by Southern Democrats, and Lodge withdrew it so as to allow the ship subsidy bill Harding favored to be debated (it was likewise filibustered). Blacks blamed Harding for the Dyer bill's defeat; Harding biographer Robert K. Murray noted that it was hastened to its end by Harding's desire to have the ship subsidy bill considered.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray_197389.E2.80.9390_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray_197389.E2.80.9390-166"><span>[</span>160<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>With the public suspicious of immigrants, especially ones who might be Socialists or Communists, Congress passed the <a href="/wiki/Emergency_Quota_Act" title="Emergency Quota Act">Per Centum Act of 1921</a>, signed by Harding on May 19, 1921, as a quick means of restricting immigration. The act reduced the numbers of immigrants to 3% of those from a given country living in the U.S., based on the 1910 census. This would, in practice, not restrict immigration from Ireland and Germany, but would bar many Italians and eastern European Jews.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESinclair215_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESinclair215-167"><span>[</span>161<span>]</span></a></sup> Harding and Secretary of Labor <a href="/wiki/James_J._Davis" title="James J. Davis">James Davis</a> believed that enforcement had to be humane, and at the secretary's recommendation, Harding allowed almost a thousand deportable immigrants to remain.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDean101.E2.80.93102_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDean101.E2.80.93102-168"><span>[</span>162<span>]</span></a></sup> Coolidge signed a bill permanently restricting immigration to the U.S. in 1924.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESinclair217_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESinclair217-169"><span>[</span>163<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Debs_and_political_prisoners">Debs and political prisoners</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Warren_G._Harding&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Debs and political prisoners">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Harding's Socialist opponent in the 1920 election, Eugene Debs, was serving a ten-year sentence in the <a href="/wiki/Atlanta_Penitentiary" title="Atlanta Penitentiary" class="mw-redirect">Atlanta Penitentiary</a> for speaking against the war. Wilson had refused to pardon him before leaving office. Daugherty met with Debs, and was deeply impressed. There was opposition from veterans, including the <a href="/wiki/American_Legion" title="American Legion">American Legion</a>, and also from Florence Harding. The president did not feel he could release Debs until the war was officially over, but once the peace treaties were signed commuted Debs' sentence on December 23, 1921. At Harding's request, Debs visited the president at the White House before going home to Indiana.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDean126.E2.80.93129_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDean126.E2.80.93129-170"><span>[</span>164<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Harding released 23 other war opponents at the same time as Debs, and continued to review cases and release political prisoners throughout his presidency. Harding defended his prisoner releases as necessary to return the nation to normalcy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969168.E2.80.93169_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969168.E2.80.93169-171"><span>[</span>165<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Judicial_appointments">Judicial appointments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Warren_G._Harding&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Judicial appointments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_federal_judges_appointed_by_Warren_G._Harding" title="List of federal judges appointed by Warren G. Harding">List of federal judges appointed by Warren G. Harding</a> and <a href="/wiki/Warren_G._Harding_Supreme_Court_candidates" title="Warren G. Harding Supreme Court candidates">Warren G. Harding Supreme Court candidates</a></div>
<p>Harding appointed four justices to the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court of the United States</a>. When Chief Justice <a href="/wiki/Edward_Douglass_White" title="Edward Douglass White">Edward Douglass White</a> died in May 1921, Harding was unsure whether to appoint former president Taft or former Utah senator <a href="/wiki/George_Sutherland" title="George Sutherland">George Sutherland</a>—he had promised seats on the court to both men. After briefly considering awaiting another vacancy and appointing them both, he chose Taft as chief justice. Sutherland was appointed to the court in 1922, to be followed by two other economic conservatives, <a href="/wiki/Pierce_Butler_(justice)" title="Pierce Butler (justice)">Pierce Butler</a> and <a href="/wiki/Edward_Terry_Sanford" title="Edward Terry Sanford">Edward Terry Sanford</a>, in 1923.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson48.E2.80.9349_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson48.E2.80.9349-172"><span>[</span>166<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Harding also appointed six judges to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Courts_of_Appeals" title="United States Courts of Appeals" class="mw-redirect">United States Courts of Appeals</a>, 42 judges to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_district_courts" title="United States district courts" class="mw-redirect">United States district courts</a>, and two judges to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Court_of_Customs_Appeals" title="United States Court of Customs Appeals" class="mw-redirect">United States Court of Customs Appeals</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173"><span>[</span>167<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Final_months.2C_death.2C_and_funeral">Final months, death, and funeral</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Warren_G._Harding&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Final months, death, and funeral">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_setbacks_and_western_tour">Political setbacks and western tour</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Warren_G._Harding&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Political setbacks and western tour">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Harding_Railroad_Car" title="Harding Railroad Car">Harding Railroad Car</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:President_Harding_in_Alaska_on_Presidential_Train.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/67/President_Harding_in_Alaska_on_Presidential_Train.jpg/220px-President_Harding_in_Alaska_on_Presidential_Train.jpg" width="220" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/67/President_Harding_in_Alaska_on_Presidential_Train.jpg/330px-President_Harding_in_Alaska_on_Presidential_Train.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/67/President_Harding_in_Alaska_on_Presidential_Train.jpg/440px-President_Harding_in_Alaska_on_Presidential_Train.jpg 2x" data-file-width="702" data-file-height="502" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:President_Harding_in_Alaska_on_Presidential_Train.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Harding aboard the presidential train in Alaska, with secretaries Hoover, Wallace, Work, and Mrs. Harding</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Entering the 1922 midterm congressional election campaign, Harding and the Republicans had followed through on many of their campaign promises. But some of the fulfilled pledges, like cutting taxes for the well-off, did not appeal to the electorate. The economy had not returned to normalcy, with unemployment at 11&#160;percent, and organized labor angry over the outcome of the strikes. From 303 Republicans elected to the House in 1920, the new <a href="/wiki/68th_United_States_Congress" title="68th United States Congress">68th Congress</a> would see that party fall to a 221–213 majority. In the Senate, the Republicans lost eight seats, and had 51 of 96 senators in the new Congress, which Harding did not survive to meet.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson80.E2.80.9381_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson80.E2.80.9381-174"><span>[</span>168<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>A month after the election, the <a href="/wiki/Lame-duck_session" title="Lame-duck session">lame-duck session</a> of the old <a href="/wiki/67th_United_States_Congress" title="67th United States Congress">67th Congress</a> met. Harding had come to believe that his early view of the presidency, that it should propose policies, but leave whether to adopt them to Congress, was not enough, and he lobbied Congress, although in vain, to get his ship subsidy bill through.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson80.E2.80.9381_174-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson80.E2.80.9381-174"><span>[</span>168<span>]</span></a></sup> Once Congress left town in early March 1923, Harding's popularity in the country began to recover. The economy was improving, and the programs of Harding's more able cabinet members, such as Hughes, Mellon, and Hoover, were showing results. Most Republicans realized that there was no practical alternative to supporting Harding in 1924.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray_197395_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray_197395-175"><span>[</span>169<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In the first half of 1923, Harding did two acts that were later said to indicate foreknowledge of death: he sold the <i>Star</i> (though undertaking to remain as a contributing editor for ten years after his presidency), and made a new will.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson172.E2.80.93173_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson172.E2.80.93173-176"><span>[</span>170<span>]</span></a></sup> Harding had long suffered occasional health problems, but when he was not experiencing symptoms he tended to eat, drink, and smoke too much. By 1919, he was aware he had a heart condition. Stress caused by the presidency and by Florence Harding's ill-health (she had a chronic kidney condition) debilitated him, and he never really recovered from an episode of influenza in January 1923. After that, Harding, an avid golfer, had difficulty completing a round. In June 1923, Ohio Senator Willis met with Harding, but brought to the president's attention only two of the five items he intended to discuss. When asked why, Willis responded, "Warren seemed so tired".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969438.E2.80.93439_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969438.E2.80.93439-177"><span>[</span>171<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In June 1923, Harding set out on a journey, which he dubbed the "Voyage of Understanding".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray_197395_175-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray_197395-175"><span>[</span>169<span>]</span></a></sup> The president planned to cross the country, go north to <a href="/wiki/Alaska_Territory" title="Alaska Territory" class="mw-redirect">Alaska Territory</a>, journey south along the West Coast, then travel by Navy ship through the Panama Canal, to Puerto Rico, and to return to Washington at the end of August.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969441_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969441-178"><span>[</span>172<span>]</span></a></sup> Harding loved to travel and had long contemplated a trip to Alaska.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson172_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson172-179"><span>[</span>173<span>]</span></a></sup> The trip would allow him to speak widely across the country, to politic and <a href="/wiki/Bloviation" title="Bloviation">bloviate</a> in advance of the 1924 campaign, and allow him some rest<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969439.E2.80.93440_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969439.E2.80.93440-180"><span>[</span>174<span>]</span></a></sup> away from Washington's oppressive summer heat.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray_197395_175-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray_197395-175"><span>[</span>169<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Harding's political advisers had given him a physically demanding schedule, even though the president had ordered it cut back.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDean147_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDean147-181"><span>[</span>175<span>]</span></a></sup> In Kansas City, Harding spoke on transportation issues; in <a href="/wiki/Hutchinson,_Kansas" title="Hutchinson, Kansas">Hutchinson, Kansas</a>, agriculture was the theme. In Denver, he spoke on Prohibition, and continued west making a series of speeches not matched by any president until Franklin Roosevelt. Harding had become a supporter of the World Court, and wanted the U.S. to become a member. In addition to making speeches, he visited <a href="/wiki/Yellowstone_National_Park" title="Yellowstone National Park">Yellowstone</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zion_National_Park" title="Zion National Park">Zion National Parks</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969442.E2.80.93443_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969442.E2.80.93443-182"><span>[</span>176<span>]</span></a></sup> and dedicated a monument on the <a href="/wiki/Oregon_Trail" title="Oregon Trail">Oregon Trail</a> at a celebration organized by venerable pioneer <a href="/wiki/Ezra_Meeker" title="Ezra Meeker">Ezra Meeker</a> and others.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDary322.E2.80.93323_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDary322.E2.80.93323-183"><span>[</span>177<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On July 5, Harding embarked on the <a href="/wiki/USS_Henderson_(AP-1)" title="USS Henderson (AP-1)">USS <i>Henderson</i></a> in Washington state. The first president to visit Alaska, he spent hours watching the dramatic landscapes from the deck of the <i>Henderson</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDean149_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDean149-184"><span>[</span>178<span>]</span></a></sup> After several stops along the coast, the presidential party left the ship at <a href="/wiki/Seward,_Alaska" title="Seward, Alaska">Seward</a> to take the <a href="/wiki/Alaska_Railroad" title="Alaska Railroad">Alaska Central Railway</a> to <a href="/wiki/McKinley_Park,_Alaska" title="McKinley Park, Alaska">McKinley Park</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fairbanks,_Alaska" title="Fairbanks, Alaska">Fairbanks</a>, where he addressed a crowd of 1,500 in 94&#160;°F (34&#160;°C) heat. The party was to return to Seward by the <a href="/wiki/Richardson_Highway" title="Richardson Highway">Richardson Trail</a> but due to Harding's fatigue, it went by train.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969446.E2.80.93447_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969446.E2.80.93447-185"><span>[</span>179<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On July 26, 1923, Harding toured <a href="/wiki/Vancouver" title="Vancouver">Vancouver</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_Columbia" title="British Columbia">British Columbia</a> as the first sitting American president to visit Canada.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186"><span>[</span>180<span>]</span></a></sup> Harding visited a golf course, but completed only six holes before being fatigued. After resting, he played the 17th and 18th holes so it would appear he completed the round. He was not successful in hiding his exhaustion; one reporter deemed him so tired a rest of mere days would not be sufficient to refresh him.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969447.E2.80.93448_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969447.E2.80.93448-187"><span>[</span>181<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In Seattle the next day, Harding kept up his busy schedule, giving a speech to 25,000 people at <a href="/wiki/Husky_Stadium" title="Husky Stadium">the stadium</a> at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Washington" title="University of Washington">University of Washington</a>. In the final speech he gave, Harding predicted statehood for Alaska.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969448_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969448-188"><span>[</span>182<span>]</span></a></sup> The president rushed through his speech, not waiting for applause by the audience.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189"><span>[</span>183<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Death_in_San_Francisco.2C_funeral.2C_and_memorial">Death in San Francisco, funeral, and memorial</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Warren_G._Harding&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Death in San Francisco, funeral, and memorial">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HardingFuneral.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/HardingFuneral.jpg/220px-HardingFuneral.jpg" width="220" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/HardingFuneral.jpg/330px-HardingFuneral.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/HardingFuneral.jpg/440px-HardingFuneral.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4601" data-file-height="3172" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HardingFuneral.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The funeral procession for President Harding passes by the front of the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Harding went to bed early in the evening of July 27, 1923. Later that night, he called for his physician, <a href="/wiki/Charles_E._Sawyer" title="Charles E. Sawyer">Charles E. Sawyer</a>, complaining of pain in the upper abdomen. Sawyer thought it was a recurrence of a dietary upset, but Dr. <a href="/wiki/Joel_T._Boone" title="Joel T. Boone" class="mw-redirect">Joel T. Boone</a> suspected a heart problem. The next day, as the train rushed to San Francisco, Harding felt better, and when they arrived on the morning of July 29, 1923, Harding insisted on walking from the train to the car, which rushed him to the <a href="/wiki/Palace_Hotel,_San_Francisco" title="Palace Hotel, San Francisco">Palace Hotel</a><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969449.E2.80.93450_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969449.E2.80.93450-190"><span>[</span>184<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SF_Gate.3B_December_9.2C_2012_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SF_Gate.3B_December_9.2C_2012-191"><span>[</span>185<span>]</span></a></sup> where he suffered a relapse. Doctors found that not only was Harding's heart causing problems, but he also had <a href="/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">pneumonia</a>, a serious matter in the days before effective antibiotics. When treated with caffeine and <a href="/wiki/Digitalis" title="Digitalis">digitalis</a>, Harding seemed to improve. He was pleased when his planned foreign policy address advocating membership in the World Court was released to the press by Hoover and received a favorable reception. By the afternoon of August 2, 1923, doctors allowed Harding to sit up in bed. That evening, about 7:30 pm, he was listening to his wife read him a flattering article about him from <i><a href="/wiki/The_Saturday_Evening_Post" title="The Saturday Evening Post">The Saturday Evening Post</a></i>, "A Calm Review of a Calm Man". When she paused to plump his pillows, he said, "That's good, read some more". As Florence Harding resumed, her husband twisted convulsively and collapsed, and she raced to get the doctors. They attempted stimulants, but were unable to revive him, and President Harding died of a <a href="/wiki/Cerebral_hemorrhage" title="Cerebral hemorrhage">cerebral hemorrhage</a> on August 2, 1923, at the age of 57.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969449.E2.80.93450_190-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969449.E2.80.93450-190"><span>[</span>184<span>]</span></a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-anb_24-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-anb-24"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Harding_Memorial_Marion_Ohio.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Harding_Memorial_Marion_Ohio.jpg/220px-Harding_Memorial_Marion_Ohio.jpg" width="220" height="160" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Harding_Memorial_Marion_Ohio.jpg/330px-Harding_Memorial_Marion_Ohio.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Harding_Memorial_Marion_Ohio.jpg/440px-Harding_Memorial_Marion_Ohio.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2166" data-file-height="1575" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Harding_Memorial_Marion_Ohio.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Harding_Tomb" title="Harding Tomb">Harding Tomb</a> in Marion</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Harding's death came as a great shock to the nation. The president was liked and admired, and the press and public had followed his illness closely, and been reassured by his apparent recovery.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969450_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969450-192"><span>[</span>186<span>]</span></a></sup> Harding was returned to his train in a casket for a journey across the nation followed closely in the newspapers. Nine million people lined the tracks as Harding's body was taken from San Francisco to Washington, D.C., and after services there, home to Marion, Ohio, for burial.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDean152.E2.80.93153_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDean152.E2.80.93153-193"><span>[</span>187<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In Marion, the body of Warren Harding was placed on a horse-drawn hearse, which was followed by President Coolidge and Chief Justice Taft, then by Harding's wife and father.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussell601.E2.80.93602_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussell601.E2.80.93602-194"><span>[</span>188<span>]</span></a></sup> They followed it through the city, past the <i>Star</i> building where the presses stood silent, and at last to the Marion Cemetery, where the casket was placed in the <a href="/wiki/Marion_Cemetery_Receiving_Vault" title="Marion Cemetery Receiving Vault">cemetery's receiving vault</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussell602_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussell602-195"><span>[</span>189<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969454_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969454-196"><span>[</span>190<span>]</span></a></sup> Harding's body, along with that of his wife who died in 1924, rests today in the <a href="/wiki/Harding_Tomb" title="Harding Tomb">Harding Tomb</a>, which was dedicated in 1931 by President Hoover.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussell633.2C_640_197-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussell633.2C_640-197"><span>[</span>191<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scandals">Scandals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Warren_G._Harding&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Scandals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Harding appointed a number of friends and acquaintances to federal positions. Some served competently, such as <a href="/wiki/Charles_E._Sawyer" title="Charles E. Sawyer">Charles E. Sawyer</a>, the Hardings' personal physician from Marion who attended to them in the White House. Sawyer had alerted Harding to the Veterans' Bureau scandal. Others proved ineffective in office, such as <a href="/wiki/Daniel_R._Crissinger" title="Daniel R. Crissinger" class="mw-redirect">Daniel R. Crissinger</a>, a Marion lawyer whom Harding made <a href="/wiki/Comptroller_of_the_Currency" title="Comptroller of the Currency" class="mw-redirect">Comptroller of the Currency</a> and later a governor of the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Reserve_Board" title="Federal Reserve Board" class="mw-redirect">Federal Reserve Board</a>, or Harding's old friend, Director of the Mint Frank Scobey, whom Trani and Wilson noted "did little damage during his tenure".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson45.E2.80.9346.2C_182_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson45.E2.80.9346.2C_182-198"><span>[</span>192<span>]</span></a></sup> Harding's brother-in-law, Heber H.
