Votaw, superintendent of federal prisons, was unable to root out the drug trade from within the facilities.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson45.E2.80.9346.2C_182_198-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson45.E2.80.9346.2C_182-198"><span>[</span>192<span>]</span></a></sup> Others of these associates, who were later dubbed the "<a href="/wiki/Ohio_Gang" title="Ohio Gang">Ohio Gang</a>", proved corrupt.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENevins256_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENevins256-199"><span>[</span>193<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Scobey_Mint_medal_obv.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Scobey_Mint_medal_obv.jpg/220px-Scobey_Mint_medal_obv.jpg" width="220" height="222" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Scobey_Mint_medal_obv.jpg/330px-Scobey_Mint_medal_obv.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Scobey_Mint_medal_obv.jpg/440px-Scobey_Mint_medal_obv.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1124" data-file-height="1136" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Scobey_Mint_medal_obv.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Harding made his friend, <a href="/wiki/Frank_E._Scobey" title="Frank E. Scobey" class="mw-redirect">Frank E. Scobey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Director_of_the_Mint" title="Director of the Mint" class="mw-redirect">Director of the Mint</a>. Medal by Chief Engraver <a href="/wiki/George_T._Morgan" title="George T. Morgan">George T. Morgan</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Most of the scandals that have marred the reputation of Harding's administration did not emerge until after his death. The Veterans' Bureau scandal was known to Harding in January 1923, but according to Trani and Wilson, "the president's handling of it did him little credit".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson181.E2.80.93182_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson181.E2.80.93182-200"><span>[</span>194<span>]</span></a></sup> Harding allowed the corrupt director of the bureau, <a href="/wiki/Charles_R._Forbes" title="Charles R. Forbes">Charles R. Forbes</a>, to flee to Europe, though he later returned and served prison time.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson182_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson182-201"><span>[</span>195<span>]</span></a></sup> Harding had learned that Daugherty's factotum at the Justice Department, <a href="/wiki/Jess_Smith" title="Jess Smith">Jess Smith</a>, was involved in corruption. The president ordered Daugherty to get Smith out of Washington and removed Smith's name from the upcoming presidential trip to Alaska. Smith committed suicide on May 30, 1923.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson179.E2.80.93180_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson179.E2.80.93180-202"><span>[</span>196<span>]</span></a></sup> It is uncertain how much Harding knew about Smith's illicit activities.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDean139.E2.80.93141_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDean139.E2.80.93141-203"><span>[</span>197<span>]</span></a></sup> Murray noted that Harding was not involved in the corruption, and did not condone it.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray_1973125.E2.80.93126_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray_1973125.E2.80.93126-204"><span>[</span>198<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Hoover, who accompanied Harding on the Western trip, later wrote that Harding asked then what Hoover would do if he knew of some great scandal, whether to publicize it or bury it. Hoover replied that Harding should publish and get credit for integrity, and asked for details. Harding stated that it had to do with Smith, but when Hoover enquired as to Daugherty's possible involvement, Harding refused to answer.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESinclair284.E2.80.93285_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESinclair284.E2.80.93285-205"><span>[</span>199<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Teapot_Dome">Teapot Dome</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Warren_G._Harding&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Teapot Dome">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Teapot_Dome_scandal" title="Teapot Dome scandal">Teapot Dome scandal</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Albert_B._Fall_c._1923.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Albert_B._Fall_c._1923.jpg/170px-Albert_B._Fall_c._1923.jpg" width="170" height="209" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Albert_B._Fall_c._1923.jpg/255px-Albert_B._Fall_c._1923.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Albert_B._Fall_c._1923.jpg/340px-Albert_B._Fall_c._1923.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3726" data-file-height="4572" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Albert_B._Fall_c._1923.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Albert_B._Fall" title="Albert B. Fall">Albert B. Fall</a>, Harding's first Secretary of the Interior and the first former cabinet member sent to prison for crimes committed in office</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The scandal which has likely done the greatest damage to Harding's reputation is <a href="/wiki/Teapot_Dome_scandal" title="Teapot Dome scandal">Teapot Dome</a>. Like most of the administration's scandals, it came to light after Harding's death, and he was not aware of the illegal aspects. Teapot Dome involved an oil reserve in Wyoming which was one of three set aside for the use of the Navy in a national emergency. There was a longstanding argument that the reserves should be developed: Wilson's first Interior Secretary, <a href="/wiki/Franklin_Knight_Lane" title="Franklin Knight Lane">Franklin Knight Lane</a>, was an advocate of this position. When the Harding administration took office, Interior Secretary Fall took up Lane's argument and in May 1921 Harding signed an executive order transferring the reserves from the Navy Department to Interior. This was done with the consent of <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_the_Navy" title="United States Secretary of the Navy">Navy Secretary</a> <a href="/wiki/Edwin_C._Denby" title="Edwin C. Denby" class="mw-redirect">Edwin C. Denby</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray_1973107_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray_1973107-206"><span>[</span>200<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson183.2C_185_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson183.2C_185-207"><span>[</span>201<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>An announcement by the Interior Department in July 1921 that <a href="/wiki/Edward_Doheny" title="Edward Doheny" class="mw-redirect">Edward Doheny</a> had been awarded a lease to drill along the edges of a naval reserve, <a href="/wiki/Elk_Hills_Oil_Field" title="Elk Hills Oil Field">Elk Hills</a> in California, attracted little controversy as the oil would have been lost to wells on adjacent private land.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENoggle254.E2.80.93256_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENoggle254.E2.80.93256-208"><span>[</span>202<span>]</span></a></sup> Wyoming Senator <a href="/wiki/John_B._Kendrick" title="John B. Kendrick">John Kendrick</a> had heard from constituents that Teapot Dome had also been leased, but no announcement had been made. When the Interior Department refused to provide documentation, he secured the passage of a Senate resolution compelling disclosure. The department sent a copy of the lease granting drilling rights to <a href="/wiki/Harry_Ford_Sinclair" title="Harry Ford Sinclair">Harry Sinclair</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Mammoth_Oil_Company" title="Mammoth Oil Company" class="mw-redirect">Mammoth Oil Company</a>, along with a statement that there had been no competitive bidding because military preparedness was involved—Mammoth was to build oil tanks for the Navy as part of the deal. This satisfied most people, but some conservationists such as <a href="/wiki/Gifford_Pinchot" title="Gifford Pinchot">Gifford Pinchot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harry_A._Slattery" title="Harry A. Slattery">Harry A. Slattery</a> and others disliked Fall for his pro-development views and continued to push the issue. They got Wisconsin Senator <a href="/wiki/Robert_M._La_Follette_Sr." title="Robert M. La Follette Sr.">Robert M. La Follette Sr.</a> to begin a Senate investigation into the oil leases. La Follette persuaded Democratic Montana Senator <a href="/wiki/Thomas_J._Walsh" title="Thomas J. Walsh">Thomas J. Walsh</a> to lead the investigation, and Walsh, through 1922 into 1923, read through the truckload of material provided by the Interior Department, including a letter from Harding stating that the transfer and leases had been with his knowledge and approval.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969463.E2.80.93465_209-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969463.E2.80.93465-209"><span>[</span>203<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Hearings into Teapot Dome began in October 1923, after Harding's death. Fall, who had left office earlier that year, denied receiving any money from Sinclair or Doheny; Sinclair, who also appeared, agreed. The following month, Walsh learned that Fall had spent lavishly on expanding and improving his New Mexico ranch; Fall reappeared and stated the money had come as a loan from Harding friend and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Post" title="The Washington Post">The Washington Post</a></i> publisher <a href="/wiki/Edward_B._McLean" title="Edward B. McLean" class="mw-redirect">Edward B. McLean</a>, but McLean, when he testified, denied it. Doheny told the committee that he had lent Fall the money, in cash, as a personal loan out of regard for their past association, but Fall, when compelled to appear again, <a href="/wiki/Taking_the_Fifth" title="Taking the Fifth" class="mw-redirect">invoked the Fifth Amendment</a> right against self-incrimination rather than answer questions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969465.E2.80.93471_210-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969465.E2.80.93471-210"><span>[</span>204<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Investigators found that Fall and a relative had received a total of about $400,000 from Doheny and Sinclair, and that the transfers were contemporaneous with the controversial leases.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969471.E2.80.93472_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969471.E2.80.93472-211"><span>[</span>205<span>]</span></a></sup> Fall was ultimately convicted in 1929 for accepting bribes, and in 1931, became the first U.S. cabinet member to be imprisoned for crimes committed while in office.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussell497.E2.80.93498_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussell497.E2.80.93498-212"><span>[</span>206<span>]</span></a></sup> Sinclair was convicted only of <a href="/wiki/Contempt_of_court" title="Contempt of court">contempt of court</a> for <a href="/wiki/Jury_tampering" title="Jury tampering">jury tampering</a>. Doheny, brought to trial before a jury in April 1930 for giving the bribe Fall had been convicted of accepting, was acquitted.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969472_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969472-213"><span>[</span>207<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Justice_Department">Justice Department</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Warren_G._Harding&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Justice Department">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Harry_Daugherty,_bw_photo_portrait_1920.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Harry_Daugherty%2C_bw_photo_portrait_1920.jpg/170px-Harry_Daugherty%2C_bw_photo_portrait_1920.jpg" width="170" height="240" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Harry_Daugherty%2C_bw_photo_portrait_1920.jpg/255px-Harry_Daugherty%2C_bw_photo_portrait_1920.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Harry_Daugherty%2C_bw_photo_portrait_1920.jpg/340px-Harry_Daugherty%2C_bw_photo_portrait_1920.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2573" data-file-height="3630" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Harry_Daugherty,_bw_photo_portrait_1920.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Harry_M._Daugherty" title="Harry M. Daugherty">Harry M. Daugherty</a> was implicated in the scandals but was never convicted of any offense.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Harding's appointment of Harry M. Daugherty as Attorney General received more criticism than any other. Daugherty's Ohio lobbying and back room maneuvers were not considered to qualify him for his office.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussell444_214-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussell444-214"><span>[</span>208<span>]</span></a></sup> When the scandals broke in 1923 and 1924, Daugherty's many enemies were delighted at the prospect of connecting him with the dishonesty, and assumed he had taken part in Teapot Dome, though Fall and Daugherty were not friends. In February 1924, the Senate voted to investigate the Justice Department, where Daugherty remained Attorney General.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969473.E2.80.93475_215-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969473.E2.80.93475-215"><span>[</span>209<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Democratic Montana Senator <a href="/wiki/Burton_K._Wheeler" title="Burton K. Wheeler">Burton K. Wheeler</a> was on the investigating committee and assumed the role of prosecutor when hearings began on March 12, 1924.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969478_216-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969478-216"><span>[</span>210<span>]</span></a></sup> Smith had engaged in <a href="/wiki/Influence_peddling" title="Influence peddling">influence peddling</a> before his suicide, conspiring with two other Ohioans, Howard Mannington and Fred A. Caskey, to accept payoffs from bootleggers to secure immunity from prosecution or to secure the release of liquor from government warehouses. Cannington and Caskey's residence became infamous as the <a href="/wiki/Little_Green_House_on_K_Street" title="Little Green House on K Street">Little Green House on K Street</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson180_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson180-217"><span>[</span>211<span>]</span></a></sup> Some witnesses, such as Smith's divorced wife Roxy Stinson, and corrupt former <a href="/wiki/FBI" title="FBI" class="mw-redirect">FBI</a> agent <a href="/wiki/Gaston_Means" title="Gaston Means">Gaston Means</a>, alleged that Daugherty was personally involved. Coolidge requested Daugherty's resignation when the Attorney General indicated that he would not allow Wheeler's committee access to Justice Department records, and Daugherty complied on March 28, 1924.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969478.E2.80.93479_218-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969478.E2.80.93479-218"><span>[</span>212<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The illicit activity that caused Daugherty the most problems was a Smith deal with Colonel <a href="/wiki/Thomas_W._Miller" title="Thomas W. Miller">Thomas W. Miller</a>, a former Delaware congressman, whom Harding had appointed <a href="/wiki/Alien_Property_Custodian" title="Alien Property Custodian" class="mw-redirect">Alien Property Custodian</a>. Smith and Miller received a payoff of almost half a million dollars for getting a German-owned firm, the American Metal Company, released to new U.S. owners. Smith deposited $50,000 in a <a href="/wiki/Joint_account" title="Joint account">joint account</a> with Daugherty, used for political purposes. Records relating to that account were destroyed by Daugherty and his brother. Miller and Daugherty were indicted for defrauding the government. The first trial, in September 1926, resulted in a <a href="/wiki/Hung_jury" title="Hung jury">hung jury</a>; at the second, early in 1927, Miller was convicted and served prison time, but the jury again hung as to Daugherty. Though charges against Daugherty were then dropped, and he was never convicted of any offense, his refusal to take the stand in his own defense devastated what was left of his reputation. The former Attorney General remained defiant, blaming his troubles on his enemies in the labor movement and on the Communists, and wrote that he had "done nothing that prevents my looking the whole world in the face".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson180.E2.80.93181_219-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson180.E2.80.93181-219"><span>[</span>213<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969480.E2.80.93481_220-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969480.E2.80.93481-220"><span>[</span>214<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Veterans.27_Bureau">Veterans' Bureau</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Warren_G._Harding&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Veterans' Bureau">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cforbes.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Cforbes.jpg/170px-Cforbes.jpg" width="170" height="248" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Cforbes.jpg/255px-Cforbes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Cforbes.jpg/340px-Cforbes.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1029" data-file-height="1501" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cforbes.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Charles_R._Forbes" title="Charles R. Forbes">Charles R. Forbes</a>, director of the Veterans' Bureau, who was sent to prison for defrauding the government</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Charles_R._Forbes" title="Charles R. Forbes">Charles R. Forbes</a>, the energetic director of the Veterans' Bureau, sought to consolidate control of veterans' hospitals and their construction in his bureau. At the start of Harding's presidency, this power was vested in the Treasury Department. The politically-powerful <a href="/wiki/American_Legion" title="American Legion">American Legion</a> backed Forbes and denigrated those who opposed him, like Secretary Mellon, and in April 1922, Harding agreed to transfer control to the Veterans' Bureau.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969459.E2.80.93460_221-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969459.E2.80.93460-221"><span>[</span>215<span>]</span></a></sup> Forbes' main task was to ensure that new hospitals were built around the country to help the 300,000 wounded World War I veterans.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdams287_222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdams287-222"><span>[</span>216<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Near the beginning of 1922, Forbes had met Elias Mortimer, agent for the Thompson-Black Construction Company of St. Louis, which wanted to construct the hospitals. The two men became close, and Mortimer paid for Forbes' travels through the West, looking at potential hospital sites for the wounded World War I veterans. Forbes was also friendly with Charles F. Hurley, owner of the Hurley-Mason Construction Company of Washington state.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969460_223-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969460-223"><span>[</span>217<span>]</span></a></sup> Harding had ordered that all contracts be pursuant to public notice,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussell526_224-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussell526-224"><span>[</span>218<span>]</span></a></sup> but the three worked out a deal whereby the two companies would get the contracts with the profits divided three ways. Some of the money went to the bureau's chief counsel, Charles F. Cramer.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969460_223-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969460-223"><span>[</span>217<span>]</span></a></sup> Forbes defrauded the government in this hospital construction, increasing construction costs from $3,000 to $4,000 per bed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussell525_225-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussell525-225"><span>[</span>219<span>]</span></a></sup> A tenth of the inflated construction billings was set aside for the conspirators, with Forbes receiving a third of the take.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerrell2369_226-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerrell2369-226"><span>[</span>220<span>]</span></a></sup> The graft spread to land acquisition, with Forbes authorizing the purchase of a San Francisco tract for $105,000 that was worth less than $20,000. At least $25,000 of the excess was divided between Forbes and Cramer.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969460_223-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969460-223"><span>[</span>217<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Intent on making more money, Forbes in November 1922 began selling valuable hospital supplies under his control in large warehouses at the Perryville Depot in Maryland.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdams289.2C_292_227-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdams289.2C_292-227"><span>[</span>221<span>]</span></a></sup> The government had stockpiled huge quantities of hospital supplies during the first World War, which Forbes unloaded for a fraction of their cost to the Boston firm of Thompson and Kelly at a time when the Veterans' Bureau was buying supplies for the hospitals at a much higher price.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussell524.E2.80.93525_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussell524.E2.80.93525-228"><span>[</span>222<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The check on Forbes' authority at Perryville was Dr. Sawyer, Harding's physician and chairman of the Federal Hospitalization Board.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdams232.2C_292.2C_294_229-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdams232.2C_292.2C_294-229"><span>[</span>223<span>]</span></a></sup> Sawyer told Harding that Forbes was selling valuable hospital supplies to an insider contractor.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdams294_230-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdams294-230"><span>[</span>224<span>]</span></a></sup> At first Harding did not believe it, but Sawyer secured proof in January 1923.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson182_201-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson182-201"><span>[</span>195<span>]</span></a></sup> A shocked Harding, who alternated between rage and despondency over the corruption in his administration, summoned Forbes to the White House and demanded his resignation. Harding did not want an open scandal and allowed Forbes to flee to Europe, from where he resigned on February 15, 1923. In spite of Harding's efforts, gossip about Forbes' activities resulted in the Senate ordering an investigation two weeks later,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray_1973103_231-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray_1973103-231"><span>[</span>225<span>]</span></a></sup> and in mid-March, Cramer committed suicide.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussell563_232-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussell563-232"><span>[</span>226<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Mortimer was willing to tell all, as Forbes had had an affair with his wife (which also broke up the Forbes marriage). The construction executive was the star witness at the hearings in late 1923, after Harding's death. Forbes returned from Europe to testify, but convinced few, and in 1924, he and John W. Thompson, of Thompson–Black, were tried in Chicago for conspiracy to defraud the government. Both were convicted and sentenced to two years in prison. Forbes began to serve his sentence in 1926; Thompson, who had a bad heart, died that year before commencing his.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray_1973106.E2.80.93107_233-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray_1973106.E2.80.93107-233"><span>[</span>227<span>]</span></a></sup> According to Trani and Wilson, "One of the most troublesome aspects of the Harding presidency was that he appeared to be far more concerned with political liabilities of a scandal than in securing justice."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson182_201-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson182-201"><span>[</span>195<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Extramarital_affairs">Extramarital affairs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Warren_G._Harding&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Extramarital affairs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Harding had an extramarital affair with <a href="/wiki/Carrie_Fulton_Phillips" title="Carrie Fulton Phillips">Carrie Fulton Phillips</a> of Marion, which lasted about fifteen years before ending in 1920. Letters from Harding to Phillips were discovered by Harding biographer <a href="/wiki/Francis_Russell_(author)" title="Francis Russell (author)">Francis Russell</a> in the possession of Marion attorney Donald Williamson while Russell was researching his book in 1963. Before that, the affair was not generally known. Williamson donated the letters to the <a href="/wiki/Ohio_Historical_Society" title="Ohio Historical Society" class="mw-redirect">Ohio Historical Society</a>. Some there wanted the letters destroyed to preserve what remained of Harding's reputation. A lawsuit ensued, with Harding's heirs claiming copyright over the letters. The case was ultimately settled in 1971, with the letters donated to the <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Congress" title="Library of Congress">Library of Congress</a>. They were sealed until 2014, but before their opening, historians used copies at <a href="/wiki/Case_Western_Reserve_University" title="Case Western Reserve University">Case Western Reserve University</a> and in Russell's papers at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Wyoming" title="University of Wyoming">University of Wyoming</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoffey84_234-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoffey84-234"><span>[</span>228<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussell650.E2.80.93663_235-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussell650.E2.80.93663-235"><span>[</span>229<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerrell3207_236-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerrell3207-236"><span>[</span>230<span>]</span></a></sup> Russell concluded from the letters that Phillips was the love of Harding's life&#160;— "the enticements of his mind and body combined in one person",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussell167_237-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussell167-237"><span>[</span>231<span>]</span></a></sup> but historian Justin P. Coffey in his 2014 review of Harding biographies criticizes him for "obsess[ing] over Harding's sex life".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoffey85_238-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoffey85-238"><span>[</span>232<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The allegations of Harding's other known mistress, <a href="/wiki/Nan_Britton" title="Nan Britton">Nan Britton</a>, long remained uncertain. In 1927, Britton, also a Marionite, published <i><a href="/wiki/The_President%27s_Daughter_(1928_book)" title="The President's Daughter (1928 book)">The President's Daughter</a></i>, alleging that her child <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Ann_Blaesing" title="Elizabeth Ann Blaesing">Elizabeth Ann Blaesing</a> had been fathered by Harding. The book, which was dedicated to "all unwedded mothers" and "their innocent children whose fathers are usually not known to the world", was sold, like pornography, door-to-door wrapped in brown paper.<sup id="cite_ref-Robenalt13Aug_239-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Robenalt13Aug-239"><span>[</span>233<span>]</span></a></sup> The late president's reputation had deteriorated since his death in 1923, and many believed Britton.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoffey80_240-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoffey80-240"><span>[</span>234<span>]</span></a></sup> The public was tantalized by salacious details such as Britton's claim that the two had sex in a White House closet, with <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secret_Service" title="United States Secret Service">Secret Service</a> agents posted to ward off intruders.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoffey80_240-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoffey80-240"><span>[</span>234<span>]</span></a></sup> Although part of the public believed her, a jury found against her when she alleged she was libeled by a refutation of her book.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESinclair293_241-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESinclair293-241"><span>[</span>235<span>]</span></a></sup> According to Harding family lore, the late President was infertile and could not have fathered a child, having suffered from <a href="/wiki/Mumps" title="Mumps">mumps</a> in childhood;<sup id="cite_ref-DNA_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DNA-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> Britton maintained that Harding had provided child support of $500 per month for the daughter he never met,<sup id="cite_ref-Strochlic14Aug_242-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Strochlic14Aug-242"><span>[</span>236<span>]</span></a></sup> but she had destroyed romantic correspondence from him at his request.<sup id="cite_ref-DNA_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DNA-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Strochlic14Aug_242-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Strochlic14Aug-242"><span>[</span>236<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Harding's biographers, writing while Britton's allegations remained uncertain, differed on their truth; Russell believed them unquestioningly<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoffey85_238-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoffey85-238"><span>[</span>232<span>]</span></a></sup> while Dean, having reviewed Britton's papers at <a href="/wiki/UCLA" title="UCLA" class="mw-redirect">UCLA</a>, dismissed them as unproven.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDean162_243-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDean162-243"><span>[</span>237<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2015, the results of DNA comparisons between members of the Harding and Blaesing families conducted by <a href="/wiki/Ancestry.com" title="Ancestry.com">ancestry.com</a> indicated that Harding was Elizabeth's father.<sup id="cite_ref-DNA_6-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DNA-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> Sinclair wondered that Harding's infidelity was held so much against him, given that <a href="/wiki/Grover_Cleveland" title="Grover Cleveland">Grover Cleveland</a> was elected president in 1884 although it was known he had a mistress and may have fathered a son out of wedlock.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESinclair293_241-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESinclair293-241"><span>[</span>235<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_view">Historical view</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Warren_G._Harding&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Historical view">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Warren_G_Hardiing_1923_Issue-2c.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Warren_G_Hardiing_1923_Issue-2c.jpg/170px-Warren_G_Hardiing_1923_Issue-2c.jpg" width="170" height="193" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Warren_G_Hardiing_1923_Issue-2c.jpg/255px-Warren_G_Hardiing_1923_Issue-2c.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Warren_G_Hardiing_1923_Issue-2c.jpg/340px-Warren_G_Hardiing_1923_Issue-2c.jpg 2x" data-file-width="485" data-file-height="552" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Warren_G_Hardiing_1923_Issue-2c.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Harding memorial issue, issued September&#160;1, 1923</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Upon his death, Harding was deeply mourned. He was called a man of peace in many European newspapers; American journalists praised him lavishly, with some describing him as having given his life for his country. His associates were stunned by his demise; Daugherty wrote, "I can hardly write about it or allow myself to think about it yet".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969456.E2.80.93457_244-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969456.E2.80.93457-244"><span>[</span>238<span>]</span></a></sup> Hughes stated, "I cannot realize that our beloved Chief is no longer with us".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969457_245-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969457-245"><span>[</span>239<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hagiography" title="Hagiography">Hagiographic</a> accounts of Harding's life quickly followed his death, such as Joe Mitchell Chapple's <i>Life and Times of Warren G. Harding, Our After-War President</i> (1924).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson208_246-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson208-246"><span>[</span>240<span>]</span></a></sup> By then, the scandals were breaking, and the Harding administration soon became a byword for corruption in the view of the public. Works written in the late 1920s helped shape Harding's historical reputation: <i>Masks in a Pageant</i> by <a href="/wiki/William_Allen_White" title="William Allen White">William Allen White</a> mocked and dismissed Harding, as did <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Hopkins_Adams" title="Samuel Hopkins Adams">Samuel Hopkins Adams</a>' fictionalized account of the Harding administration, <i>Revelry</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoffey80_240-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoffey80-240"><span>[</span>234<span>]</span></a></sup> These books depicted Harding's time in office as one of great presidential weakness.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerrell2970_247-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerrell2970-247"><span>[</span>241<span>]</span></a></sup> The publication of Nan Britton's bestselling book alleging they had had an affair also lowered the late president in public esteem. President Coolidge, not wishing to be further associated with his predecessor, refused to dedicate the Harding Tomb. Hoover, Coolidge's successor, was similarly reluctant, but with Coolidge in attendance presided over the dedication in 1931. By that time, with the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a> in full swing, Hoover was nearly as discredited as Harding.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussell632.E2.80.93633.2C_639.E2.80.93640_248-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussell632.E2.80.93633.2C_639.E2.80.93640-248"><span>[</span>242<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPayne125.2C_127_249-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPayne125.2C_127-249"><span>[</span>243<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Adams continued to shape the negative view of Harding with several nonfiction works in the 1930s, culminating with <i>The Incredible Era—The Life and Times of Warren G. Harding</i> (1939) in which he called his subject "an amiable, well-meaning third-rate <a href="/wiki/Babbitt_(novel)" title="Babbitt (novel)">Mr. Babbitt</a>, with the equipment of a small-town semi-educated journalist&#160;... It could not work. It did not work."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson209_250-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson209-250"><span>[</span>244<span>]</span></a></sup> Dean deems the works of White and Adams "remarkably unbalanced and unfair accounts, exaggerating the negative, assigning responsibility to Harding for all wrongs, and denying him credit for anything done right. Today there is considerable evidence refuting their portrayals of Harding. Yet the myth has persisted."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDean163_251-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDean163-251"><span>[</span>245<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:President_Warren_G._Harding_and_First_Lady_Florence_Harding.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/President_Warren_G._Harding_and_First_Lady_Florence_Harding.jpg/170px-President_Warren_G._Harding_and_First_Lady_Florence_Harding.jpg" width="170" height="234" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/President_Warren_G._Harding_and_First_Lady_Florence_Harding.jpg/255px-President_Warren_G._Harding_and_First_Lady_Florence_Harding.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/President_Warren_G._Harding_and_First_Lady_Florence_Harding.jpg/340px-President_Warren_G._Harding_and_First_Lady_Florence_Harding.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1072" data-file-height="1474" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:President_Warren_G._Harding_and_First_Lady_Florence_Harding.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
President Harding and<br />
First Lady <a href="/wiki/Florence_Harding" title="Florence Harding">Florence Harding</a>.<br />
Photo c. 1922.<br />
Mrs. Harding was highly protective of her husband's personal legacy.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The opening of Harding's papers for research in 1964 sparked a small spate of biographies, of which the most controversial was Russell's <i>The Shadow of Blooming Grove</i> (1968), which concluded that the rumors of black ancestry (the "shadow" of the title) deeply affected Harding in his formative years, causing both Harding's conservatism and his desire to get along with everyone. Coffey faults Russell's methods, and deems the biography "largely critical, though not entirely unsympathetic".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoffey86_252-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoffey86-252"><span>[</span>246<span>]</span></a></sup> Murray's <i>The Harding Era</i> (1969) took a more positive view of the president, and put him in the context of his times. Trani and Wilson faulted him for "a tendency to go overboard" in trying to connect Harding with the successful policies of cabinet officers, and for asserting, without sufficient evidence, that a new, more assertive Harding had emerged by 1923.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson211_253-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrani_.26_Wilson211-253"><span>[</span>247<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>More recently, there have been revisionist books on Harding. Robert Ferrell's <i>The Strange Deaths of President Harding</i> (1996), according to Coffey, "spends almost the entire work challenging every story about Harding and concludes that almost everything that is read and taught about his subject is wrong".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoffey88.E2.80.9389_254-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoffey88.E2.80.9389-254"><span>[</span>248<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2004, John Dean, noted for his involvement in another presidential scandal, <a href="/wiki/Watergate_scandal" title="Watergate scandal">Watergate</a>, wrote the Harding volume in "The American Presidents" series of short biographies, edited by <a href="/wiki/Arthur_M._Schlesinger,_Jr." title="Arthur M. Schlesinger, Jr.">Arthur M. Schlesinger, Jr.</a>. Coffey deemed that book the most revisionist to date, and faults Dean for glossing over some unfavorable episodes in Harding's life, like his silence during the 1914 Senate campaign, when his opponent Hogan was being attacked for his faith.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoffey89_255-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoffey89-255"><span>[</span>249<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Harding has traditionally been <a href="/wiki/Historical_rankings_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="Historical rankings of Presidents of the United States">ranked as one of the worst presidents</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerrell3474.E2.80.933485_256-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerrell3474.E2.80.933485-256"><span>[</span>250<span>]</span></a></sup> In a 1948 poll conducted by <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a>, historian <a href="/wiki/Arthur_M._Schlesinger,_Sr." title="Arthur M. Schlesinger, Sr.">Arthur M. Schlesinger, Sr.</a> conducted the first notable survey of scholars' opinions of the presidents, Harding ranked last among the 29 presidents considered.<sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257"><span>[</span>251<span>]</span></a></sup> He has also been last in other polls since, which Ferrell attributes to scholars reading little but sensational accounts of Harding.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerrell3474.E2.80.933485_256-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerrell3474.E2.80.933485-256"><span>[</span>250<span>]</span></a></sup> Murray argued that Harding deserves more credit than historians have given, "he was certainly the equal of a <a href="/wiki/Franklin_Pierce" title="Franklin Pierce">Franklin Pierce</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Johnson" title="Andrew Johnson">Andrew Johnson</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Harrison" title="Benjamin Harrison">Benjamin Harrison</a>, or even a Calvin Coolidge. In concrete accomplishments, his administration was superior to a sizable portion of those in the nation's history."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969536_258-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969536-258"><span>[</span>252<span>]</span></a></sup> Coffey believes "the academic lack of interest in Harding has cost him his reputation, as scholars still rank Harding as nearly dead last among presidents".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoffey86_252-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoffey86-252"><span>[</span>246<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Murray argued that Harding sowed the seeds for his administration's poor standing:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>In the American system, there is no such thing as an innocent bystander in the White House. If Harding can rightly claim the achievements of a Hughes in State or a Hoover in Commerce, he must also shoulder responsibility for a Daugherty in Justice and a Fall in Interior. Especially must he bear the onus of his lack of punitive action against such men as Forbes and Smith. By his inaction, he forfeited whatever chance he had to maintain the integrity of his position and salvage a favorable image for himself and his administration. As it was, the subsequent popular and scholarly negative verdict was inevitable, if not wholly deserved.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969536_258-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray_1969536-258"><span>[</span>252<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Warren_G._Harding&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-17"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-17">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Kling was determined that his daughter be able to make a living if it became necessary, and so sent her to the <a href="/wiki/Cincinnati_Conservatory_of_Music" title="Cincinnati Conservatory of Music">Cincinnati Conservatory of Music</a>. After their estrangement, it became necessary. See <a href="#CITEREFDean">Dean</a>, p.&#160;15.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-20"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-20">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Harding apparently never knew with certainty whether he had black ancestry, telling a reporter, "One of my ancestors may have jumped the fence."<sup id="cite_ref-Baker18Aug_7-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baker18Aug-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-76"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-76">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Although Harding did not invent the word "normalcy", he is credited with popularizing it. See <a href="#CITEREFRussell">Russell</a>, p.&#160;347. The other word that Harding popularized was <a href="/wiki/Bloviation" title="Bloviation">bloviate</a>, which he said was a somewhat-obsolete term used in Ohio meaning to sit around and talk. After Harding's resurrection of it, it came to mean empty oratory. See <a href="#CITEREFDean">Dean</a>, p.&#160;37.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-102"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-102">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Mencken nevertheless voted for Harding. See <a href="#CITEREFSinclair">Sinclair</a>, p.&#160;165.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-114"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-114">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Harding resigned from the Senate in January 1921, waiting until Cox's term as governor expired. A Republican governor, <a href="/wiki/Harry_L._Davis" title="Harry L. Davis">Harry L. Davis</a>, appointed Willis, already elected to a full term on Harding's <a href="/wiki/Coattail_effect" title="Coattail effect">coattails</a>, to serve the remainder of Harding's term. See <a href="#CITEREFDean">Dean</a>, p.&#160;92.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-133"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-133">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">By Hughes's departure from office in 1925, American forces had left the Dominican Republic and were about to leave Nicaragua. The departure from Haiti was still being planned. See <a href="#CITEREFTrani_.26_Wilson">Trani &amp; Wilson</a>, p.&#160;135.</span></li>
</ol>
</div>
